<page sig="a1r">
<section type="Title-page">
AN 
ENGLISH 
EXPOSITOR: TEACHING THE INTER&shy;
pretation of the hardest words vsed 
in our Language. WITH SVNDRY EXPLICATIONS, 
Descriptions, and Discourses.

By I. B. Doctor of Physick&egrave;.

<term lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03C1;&#x03B3;&#x03BF;<damage></damage></term>

LONDON 
Printed by IOHN LEGATT. 
1621.
</section>
</page>

<page sig="a1v">
</page>

<page sig="a2r">
<section type="Dedication">
TO THE RIGHT 
HONORABLE AND VER&shy;
TVOVS, HIS SINGVLAR 
GOOD LADIE, THE LADIE 
IANE Vicountesse Mountague, all 
honour and happinesse.

BEing perswaded (Right 
Noble Lady) by some 
friends, for publike be&shy;
nefit to make this colle&shy;
ction of words common, 
which at first was inten&shy;
ded only for priuate vse, 
(as written in my youth, at the request of a 
worthy Gentleman, one whose loue preuai&shy;
led much with me) I could not find in heart 
to send it forth, no better furnished than with 
a bare Title; least like an vnknowne Infant, it 
should be exposed to ouer hard vsage, or per&shy;
aduenture scornefully reiected, aduenturing 
abroad without countenance of any friend 
or commander. Vpon sure knowledge there&shy;
</page>

<page sig="a2v">
fore of your most honourable disposition, 
and former experience of your Ladyships 
fauour toward me, I am emboldned to pre&shy;
sent this little Pamphlet vnto your honour, 
with hope that by your Patronage it shall 
not onely be protected from iniuries, but al&shy;
so finde fauourable entertainment, and per&shy;
haps gracefully admitted among greatest 
Ladies & studious Gentlewomen, to whose 
reading (I am made beleeue) it will not 
proue altogether vngratefull. And although 
I may well be taxed of folly or presumption, 
that for credit of a slender triflle, craue the 
assistance of so eminent a person, yet such (I 
confesse) is my care thereof (since I must not 
deny it for my owne) and my duty and de&shy;
uotion so affected to your honour, and all 
yours; that I haue, (hoping of your Ladiships 
pardon) wilfully repelled all reasons which 
might disswade me, choosing herein not so 
much what may seeme most seemely, as 
what I suppose most behoouefull for me.

Accept therefore I beseech your Honour, 
and receiue vnder your noble tuition this lit&shy;
tle vocabulary Treatise, which hauing beene 
many years restrained of liberty, is now glad 
of enlargement, especially recommended vn&shy;
to so worthy a Patronesse. The glorious Sun 
</page>

<page sig="a3r">
loseth not the least point of his heighth by 
liberall lending downe his light, and quick&shy;
ning with his influence the lowest creatures; 
neither is any eminency disgraced, that easi&shy;
ly condescendeth to others desired good: 
which is also confirmed by the Poet, who 
saieth;

<term lang="la">Conspicitur nunquam meliore potentia causa,
Qu&agrave;m quoties vanas non sinit esse preces.</term>

Not thinking needfull therfore to trouble 
your Honour with many words of entreaty 
(vpon assured confidence of your wel known 
Charity and goodnesse) I conclude, sincere&shy;
ly wishing to your Ladiship, the best that 
may be wished, and resting my selfe euer.

Your Honours to be 
commanded, 

IO. BVLLOKAR.
</section>
</page>

<page sig="a3v">
<section type="To the Reader">
To the Courteous Reader.

HEre haue you (Gentle Reader) that 
which at first was not made for you, 
though now willingly (for your be&shy;
nefite if you embrace it) offered to 
your kind acceptance. Commend it 
my selfe I will not, lest I should giue 
occasion to some quick heads, to come 
vpon me with the verse, <term lang="la">Autor o&shy;
pus laudat,</term> &c. Yet this I will say 
(and say truely) that in my yonger yeares it hath cost mee 
some obseruation, reading, study, and charge; which you may 
easily beleeue, considering the great store of strange words, our 
speech doth borrow, not only from the Latine, and Greeke, (and 
some from the ancient Hebrew) but also from forraine vulgar 
Languages round about vs: beside sundry olde words now 
growne out of vse, and diuers termes of art, proper to the lear&shy;
ned in Logicke, Philosophy, Law, Physicke, Astronomie, &c. 
yea, and Diuinitie it selfe, best knowen to the seuerall profes&shy;
sors thereof. And herein I hope such learned will deeme no 
wrong offered to themselues or dishonour to Learning, in that 
I open the signification of such words, to the capacitie of the 
ignorant, whereby they may conceiue and vse them as well as 
those which haue bestowed long study in the languages; for 
considering it is familiar among best writers to vsurpe 
strange words, (and sometime necessary be reason our speech 
is not sufficiently furnished with apt termes to expresse all 
</page>

<page sig="a4r">
meanings) I suppose withall their desire is that they should 
also be vnderstood; which I (knowing that <term lang="la">bonum qu&ograve;
communius eo melius</term>) haue endeauoured by this Booke, 
though not exquisitely, yet (I trust) in some reasonable 
measure to performe. It is easier (all know) to find faults, 
then to mende them, and easier to mend faults in anothers 
worke already written, then to write any new worke free from 
all fault. If therefore any fault finder, or ouer curious Cri&shy;
ticke (for from the best learned I expect best vsage) shall to 
shew his skill grow captious, and quarrell at my interpretati&shy;
ons, I will desire him to forbeare bitternesse, and temper a 
while his choler, till hauing laide this worke aside, hee trauell 
himselfe in the same or some other new argument; and then I 
doubt not but he will either become more indifferent, or giue 
others occasion to bid him English the olde Prouerbe, <term lang="la">Medice 
cura teipsum</term>. But as for you (iudiciall or courteous Reader) 
whose fauour I desire, and whose counsell or friendly correcti&shy;
on I will not refuse, if to you (I say) any thing herein shall 
occurre, which seemeth by me omitted, mistaken, or not fully 
satisfactory to your expectation, (for indeed there are almost 
slipt away seuen years since I had any leasure as much as to 
looke on it) I promise that vpon warning hereof giuen to me 
or the Printer, at a second Impression it shall be amended or 
supplyed. Meanewhile vse this as you finde it, and I perswade 
my selfe your honesty will say it is worth the money you 
paide for it. So committing my selfe and Booke to your fauo&shy;
rable good liking, I commit you to God. From my house 
at Chicester in Sussex, this 17. day of October. 1616.

Your hearty well-willer, 

Io: Bullokar.
</section>
</page>

<page sig="a4v">
<section type="Instruction to the Reader">
An Instruction to the Reader.

HAue care to search euery word ac&shy;
cording to the true Orthography 
thereof, as for Ph&oelig;nix in the Letter 
P. not in F. for Hypostaticall in Hy: not in 
Hi: Remember also that euery word mar&shy;
ked with this marke * is an olde word, one&shy;
ly vsed of some ancient writers, and now 
growne out of vse. Lastly, if a word bee of 
different significations, the one easie, the o&shy;
ther more difficult, I onely speake of inter&shy;
pretation of the hardest; as in the words 
Tenne, Girle, Garter, may appeare.
</section>
</page>

<subform lang="en">1. Action personall.</subform> <subxpln>which is for debt, goods, cattell.</subxpln>
<subform lang="en">2. Action popular:</subform> <subxpln>which any man may sue, as vpon the breach of a penall sta&shy;
tute, where some aduan&shy;
tage is allowed to him that will sue for it.</subxpln>
<subform lang="en">3. Action reall:</subform> <subxpln>when one claimeth title to any lands tenements, rents, or com&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="adamant(n)">Adamant.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A precious stone commonly called a Diamond, brought out of Arabia and Cyprus. It is the hardest of all stones, insomuch that it cutteth glasse, and yieldeth neither to stroke of hammer nor fire, for which cause the Greekes name it Adamas, which in their tongue sig&shy;
nifieth Inuincible: Not&shy;
withstanding it is softened with goates blood, beeing steeped therein new and warme. It is of contrary nature to the loadstone, in so much that beeing laide neere it, the loadstone can not draw yron, hauing the vertue thereof ouer maste&shy;
red
</page>

<page sig="b3r">
by the Adamant.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="adjunct(n)">Adiunct.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A qualitie ioyned to a thing, as heate to fire, coldnesse to Snow.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="alleluia(ex)">Alleluia.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An Hebrew word or rather two Hebrewe words ioyned in one, vsed as a signe of exultation, and is interpreted, Prayse ye our Lord. Paulus Diaco&shy;
nus writeth, that when the Britaines were inuaded by the Saxons and Picts, and on a time ready to fight a battell against them, they were admonished by Ger&shy;
manus a French Bishop, (who was sent hither with Saint Lupus to confute the Pelagian heresie) that they should doe as he did; and forthwith he cryed aloude Alleluia: which when the whole armie of Britaines had done, the sound there&shy;
of strooke such a terrour into the enemies, that they presently fledde a&shy;
way, whereby the Bri&shy;
taines had the victorie. <term lang="la">De gestis Rom. lib.</term> 15.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aloe(n)">Aloes,</form> <xpln lang="en"> or Lignum Aloes. A precious wood vsed in Phisicke, which comfor&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="amazon(n)">Amazon.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A woman of the Country Amazonica. Amazones were warlike women of Scythia, which kept a Countrey to them&shy;
selues without men, yet to haue children companied with the bordering people. Their Sonnes they either destroyed or sent home to the father, but their daugh&shy;
ters they kept, bringing them vp in hunting, ri&shy;
ding, shooting and feates of armes. They burned the right breast of their children, lest it should hin&shy;
der their archerie, where&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ark(n)">Arke.</form> <xpln lang="en"> In holy Scripture it signifieth two things: First, The Arke made by Noe at the commandement of God, which was 300. cu&shy;
bits long: 50, cubits broad, and 30. high: Gen. 6. Se&shy;
condly, it signifieth a most precious and consecrated cofer or chest, called the Arke of testament, made of the wood Sethim, and plated within and without all ouer with gold: It had foure corners, and in each corner a golden ring, tho&shy;
rough which were put barres of the same wood Sethim, couered likewise with gold, which serued for the carriage thereof. This Arke was two cubits and a halfe long, one cubit and a halfe broad, and one cubit &amp; a half deepe. Exod. 25. In it was kept part of the Manna in a pot of gold, also the two Tables of the Law, and Aarons rod that had budded: Heb. 9.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="armadillo(n)">Armadilio.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A beast in India of the bignesse of a yong pigge, couered ouer with small shels like vnto armour; for which cause he is called Armadilio, to wit, an armed beast. This beast liueth in the ground like a mole, and the bone of his taile hath vertue to cure diseases and noise in the head.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="asp(n)">Aspe.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A venemous ser&shy;
pent of a blacke earthie colour and sometime yel&shy;
low. The superstitious E&shy;
gyptians did honor them, and their Kings vsed to weare the figure of an aspe in their Diadems. They go alwaies two and two toge&shy;
ther, and if it happen that one of them be killed, the other will presently pursue him that slue his fellow, in such sort that he shal hard&shy;
ly escape, vnlesse hee make great hast or passe ouer a riuer. If one bee bitten by this serpent, the best re&shy;
medy is presently to cutte off the member so bitten, if it be Possible; otherwise hee shall fall into a deadly sleep, &amp; within few houres goe away as it were in a trance, Cleopatra Queene of Egypt, after the death of Marcus Antonius (wh<expan type="o+_">om</expan>?
</page>

<page sig="c2v">
she loued as her husband) slue her selfe wilfully, by applying one of these asps to her body; because she would not bee carryed in triumph to Rome, by Augustus Caesar, who had van&shy;
quished her and Antonie.
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aspect(n)">Aspect.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Sight or the be&shy;
holding of any thing. In Astronomy it signifieth the distance betweene the planets &amp; heauenly signes And there are foure such Aspects. The first called a Trine aspect (because it diuideth the heauens into three euen partes) is the distance of foure signes from each other; as Aries beholdeth Leo, and Sagit&shy;
tarius with a Trine aspect, because these are distant foure signes, the one be&shy;
fore, the other after Aries. The second called a Quar&shy;
till, is the distance of three signes, as Aries beholdeth Cancer and Capricorne, with a Quartill aspect, because they are distant three signes from him. The third called a Sextill as&shy;
pect, is the distance of two signes, as Aries beholdeth Gemini and Aquarius with this Sextill aspect, beeing but two signes distant from them. The fourth called an Opposite aspect, is the farthest distance that can be, namely a distance of sixe signes asunder; as Aries beholdeth Libra with this opposite aspect, and Libra beholdeth Aries with the same. The like is of all the other signes, or Planets placed in them, For example; Taurus be&shy;
holdeth Cancer and Pisces with a Sextill, Leo and A&shy;
quarius with a Quartill. Virgo and Capricorne with a Trine, and Scorpio with an opposite aspect. The di&shy;
stance of one or fiue signes is not called an Aspect.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="balm(n)">Balme.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A precious iuyce or liquor, otherwise called Balsamum, or Opobalsa&shy;
mum. It droppeth by cut&shy;
ting out of a little lowe plant (about a yard high) hauing leaues like Rue, but whiter, which plant grow&shy;
eth in Egypt, and some places of the holy Land. This iuice is somewhat like to oyle, but more clammie, and inclining to a certaine rednesse. It hath a strong smell, and is not pleasant in taste: Being put into a vessell of water it will sinke downe to the bottome like a round pearle, without breaking and may bee taken vp a&shy;
gaine with the point of a knife. It is an excellent medicine to take any scar out of the body, and for diuers other purposes, but very costly and rarely got&shy;
</page>

<page sig="c5r">
ten Saladinus writes that there was but one vine&shy;
yard of these in the whole world, and that belonged to the great Turke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="basilisk(n)">Basiliske.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Otherwise cal&shy;
led a Cockatrise: the most venemous serpent that is. It breaketh stones and blasteth all plants with the breath thereof, burning e&shy;
uery thing that it goeth o&shy;
uer; neither can any herbe growe neere the place where it lyeth. It is poy&shy;
son to poyson, and driueth away all other serpents, with only hissing. If a man touch it but with a sticke, it will kill him, and if it see a man a farre off, it de&shy;
stroyeth him with his lookes. This serpent is not aboue a foote long, of co&shy;
lour between black &amp; yel&shy;
low, hauing red eye, a ve&shy;
ry sharpe head, and a white
</page>

<page sig="c5v">
spot thereon like a crowne wherefore he is called by some in Latine <term lang="la">Regulus</term>, viz. a little king. It goeth not winding like other serpents, but vpright vnto the middle, holding vp the brest thereof. It breedeth onely in the hot burning sands of Africa: Of this Basiliske the Poet Lucane writeth thus, <term lang="la">lib.</term> 9.

<lemeformat>
<term lang="la">Sibilaque effundens cunctas terrentia pestes.
Ante venena nocens, late sibi submouet omne
Vulgus, &amp; in vacua regnat Basiliscus arena.</term>
With deadly hisse the Basiliske,
  all other plagues doth fright.
And speedier kils then poisons can,
  with his infectious sight.
Heele haue no neighbour dwell neere him,
  he loues to liue alone.
And tyrantlike reignes by himselfe,
  in caue of sandy stone.
</lemeformat>
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bdellium(n)">Bdellium.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Gumme brought out of Arabia, and the Holy land, of a sweete smell and bitter taste. It hath vertue to mollifie and ripen hard swellings, and is good against the stiffe&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bead(n)" lexeme="Saint Elline(pn)">Beades of Saint Elline.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Cer&shy;

</page>

<page sig="c6r">
are of great vertue against griefs of the stomack, as al&shy;
so of the kidnies or reines.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="beaver(n)">Beauer.</form> <xpln lang="en"> In armour it sig&shy;
nifieth that part of the hel&shy;
met which may bee lifted vp, to take breath the more freely. It is also a beast of very hotte nature, liuing much in the water. His two forefeete are like the feete of the beast called Gattus, (as Ioannes de San&shy;
cto amando writeth:) but what this Gattus is, I doe not well vnderstand, onely I suppose it to be an Otter. Vpon these feete hee hath very sharpe clawes, where&shy;
with hee taketh his prey, and hangeth vpon water bankes. His hinder feet are made like to the foot of a Goose, wherewith hee swimmeth. Hee hath very sharpe teeth, and doeth much harme to trees, with knawing the rootes and ryndes of them. It is writ&shy;
ten that if a Beauer come into a strange place, where hee hath not beene bred, the other Beauers will make him their slaue, to prouide them meate, and pull off all the haire from his backe that hee may be knowne. The stones of this beast are sold in Apo&shy;
thecaries shoppes, by the name of Castoreum: they are much vsed in Physick, beeing very good against palsies and cold diseases of the sinewes: But the skin is of more valew then the stones.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bee-glue(n)">Beeglue.</form> <xpln lang="en"> That which Bees doe make at the en&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="beryl(n)">Berill.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A precious stone brought out of India, cut most commonly with sixe corners, because otherwise it would not shew so faire, if the sticking out of the corners did not make the brightnesse more mani&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bezoar-stone(n)">Bezar stone.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A stone of excellent vertue against poyson, very costly and of great account in Physick. It is commonly of the bignesse of an Acorne or Chestnut, being compo<expan type="u+_">un</expan>&shy;
ded of certain thin scales, one vpon another, like the scales of an Onion. It is easie to bee scraped or cutte, and if it continue long in water, it melteth. The middle part is some&shy;
thing hollow, and full of
</page>

<page sig="c7r">
powder of the same sub&shy;
stance that the stone is of. And this is a marke to know whether the stone bee fine and true: for the Indians doe counterfeite them sometimes and de&shy;
ceiue many. This stone it taken out of the bow&shy;
els of a beast in India, much like a Hart, sauing that his hornes are like a goates. The occasion of the growing of these stones (as some write) is thus. These Harts of wilde Goates (for they resem&shy;
ble both) going to the dennes of Serpents in those countries, doe with their breathing compell them to come forth, and then eate them: after they goe whereas water is, and plunge themselues therein vntil they perceiue the furie of the venome be past, and till then they will not drinke a droppe: beeing come foorth of the water, they goe into the fields, where feeding vpon many healthfull hearbs (knowne to them by naturall instinct to bee of vertue against poyson) they are perfectly freed from all danger; and by the mixture of these herbs with the Serpents eaten before, these Bezar stones are very strangely (as is saide) ingendered within them: growing by little and little, as appeareth by the scales thereof one folded vpon an o&shy;
ther.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bezil(n)">Bezill.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The broad part of a ring, in which the stone or signet is set.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bisextile(n)">Bissextile.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Leape yeare, so called, because the sixt Calends of March are in
</page>

<page sig="c7v">
that yeare twice reckoned: viz. on the 24. and 25. of February, so that leape yeare hath one day more then other yeares haue. This leape yeare is obser&shy;
ued euery fourth yeare, &amp; was first deuised by Iulius Caesar to accommodate or make the yeare agree with the course of the sunne.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bitumen(n)">Bitumen.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A kind of clay, naturally cl<expan type="a+_">am</expan>my like pitch, growing in some coun&shy;
tries of Asia. It was of old vsed in Physicke: The best is heauy, bright, and cleere, of purple colour, and ha&shy;
uing a strong smell. The black is accounted naught. This Bitumen was vsed in stead of morter, at the building of the tower of Babel, as appeareth in Gen. cap. 11. There is also a kind of Bitumen, like vnto a liquor, flowing out of some fountaines in the yland Sicilie, which is vsed in stead of oyle to burne in lampes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="calends(n)">Calends.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It signifieth pro&shy;
perly, the first day of euery moneth, being spoken a&shy;
lone by it selfe. If Pridie be placed before it, then it signifieth the last day of the moneth going before, as <term lang="la">Pridie Calend. Ianuarij.</term> is the last day of Decem&shy;
ber. If any number hee placed with it, it signifieth that day in the former moneth, which commeth so much before the mo&shy;
neth named; as the tenth Calends of March is the twentieth day of February, because if one beginne at March, and reckon backe&shy;
wards, that twentieth day is the tenth day before March. In March, May, Iuly, and October, the Calends, beginne at the sixteenth day, in other moneths at the fourteenth which Calends must euer beare the name of the moneth following, and be numbred backeward from the first day of the saide following moneths.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="canker(n)">Canker.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A hard swelling in the veines, being ouer&shy;
charged with hot melan&shy;
choly humours. It is cal&shy;
led a Canker, because the veines so swollen are like vnto the clawes of a Crab. This disease may happen in any part of the bodie, but most commonly in womens breasts; by reason of their spongie hollow&shy;
nesse, and great number of veines their meeting. If it continue long it is very hard to cure, because gen&shy;
tle medicines will doe no good, and strong doe in&shy;
crease the raging malice of it.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="carbuncle(n)">Carbuncle.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It hath two significations, namely a precious stone, and a dan&shy;
gerous sore. 1. Carbun&shy;
cle stone, is bright, of the colour of fire. It hath ma&shy;
ny vertues, but chiefly preuaileth against the dan&shy;
ger of infectious aire. The best of these stones will shine in darkeness, like a burning coale, as Albertus writeth, himselfe hath seene. Others shine but a little, and are lesse estee&shy;
med; but such as shine not
</page>

<page sig="d2v">
at all, are scarce of any rec&shy;
koning: these stones are found in some countreies of Africa. 2. Carbuncle disease, is a botch or vlcer, (otherwise called by a Greeke name Anthrax) caused of grosse hot blood which raiseth blisters, and burneth the skinne. This vlcer is euer accompanied with a Feuer.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cassia(n)">Casia.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It is commonly taken for Cinnamome. Poets vnderstand often by it, some sweete smelling herbe: as Virg. Eclog. 2.

<lemeformat>
<term lang="la">Tum Casia atque alijs intexens suanibus herbis
Also Ovid. Quo simul ac Casias &amp; nardilenis aristas.
Quassaque cum fulua substrauit Cinnama myrrha.
Lictantius also: De Phoenice.
Cinnama dehint, auramque procul spirantis amomi
Congerit &amp; mixto balsama cum folio.
Non Casia mitis, nec olentis vinuen acanthi,
Nec thuris Lachrymae, guttaque pinguis abest.</term>
</lemeformat>

Where wee see that Casia and Cinnamome, signifie two things. Some think it to bee Lauender.

For mine owne part, I know not certainely what English name or interpre&shy;
tation to giue it.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="climacterical(a)">Climactericall.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Greek word signifying an acco<expan type="u+_">un</expan>t or reckoning, made by certaine degrees or steps. Some haue heereby diui&shy;
ded the age of mans life after this manner. The seuenth yeare they reckon for dangerous; and by this account the 14.21.28.35. &amp;c. are climactericall yeares. Likewise the ninth yeare is esteemed equally as dangerous, and by this account, the 18.27 36. &amp;c. are called climacteri&shy;
call yeares. But the most noted and famous clima&shy;
ctericall yeare, is at the age of 63. because both ac&shy;
counts doe meete in this number; namely 7. times 9. and 9. times 7. And this is held the most dangerous yeare of all other.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="coloquintida(n)">Coloquintida.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A kinde of wilde Ground, which the Persians name Gall of the earth, because it de&shy;
stroyeth all hearbes, neere which it groweth. It is round, hauing a thinne yellow rinde (when it is ripe) and the inner part open, and spongie full of gray seede. If there grow but one vpon a plant, it must bee throwne away, because it will bee too violent in operation. This fruite is often vsed in phy&shy;
sick to purge slimie grosse humours from the sin&shy;
newes and ioynts; but must be well prepared, for that otherwise it is very dangerous.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="colossus(n)">Colosse.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An image or sta&shy;
tue of exceeding great&shy;
nesse, made sometime by the olde Emperours of Rome. There was one such image in the yle of Rhodes, dedicated to the sunne, of one hundred and fiue foot long, the thun be of which image fewe men could fathome.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="consul(n)">Consull.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A chiefe officer among the Romans: there were two chosen yearly to gouerne the City: These

<page sig="e2r">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="critical(a)">Criticall.</form> <xpln lang="en"> In Phisicke the fourth and seuenth dayes are called Critical, because in them Phisitians vse to iudge of the danger of a disease: But the seuenth is accounted the chiefe Criticall day, &amp; the fourth a token or signe what the seuenth day will be, if the Patient liue so long. This account must bee made according to the number of weekes thus. In the first weeke the fourth day is the token or Critical of the
</page>

<page sig="e4v">
seuenth day. In the second weeke the eleuenth is the Critical of the fourteenth. In the third the seuentieth is the Critticall of the twentieth: for Hippocrates reckoneth the twentieth day for the last to the third weeke: In the fourth week 24. is the Criticall of the 27. In the fifty the 31. the Criticall of the 34. In the sixt weeke the 37. the Cri&shy;
ticall of the 40. And so foorth to a hundred.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="critic(n)">Criticke.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The same that Criticall is. Also it signi&shy;
fieth sometime, one that hath authoritie, or taketh vpon him to censure other mens acts or workes writ&shy;
ten.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="crocodile(n)">Crocodile.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A harmefull beast liuing most about the Riuer Nilus in Aegypt. It is hatched of an egge, and groweth vnto a won&shy;
derfull greatnesse, some&shy;
time to twentie or thirtie foot long. This beast hath no tongue, and in feeding mooueth onely the vpper iawe: He hath eyes like a Hogge, and cruell sharpe teeth: Hee hath no haire but is made much after the fashion of an Euet, with a long taile, a long belly, and backe couered all ouer with scales close ioyned &amp; of great strength. Vpon his feete hee hath strong sharpe clawes. In the day time hee can see farre, and liueth on land, in the night he is almost blind, and keepeth in the water. He is very bold vp&shy;
on those he seeth afraid of him, and fearefull if he be assaulted. It is written that he will weepe ouer a mans head, when he hath deuou&shy;
red the body, and then will eate vp the head two. Wherefore in Latine there is a <emend err="probere">proverbe</emend>, <term lang="la">Crocodili Ia&shy;
chrym&aelig;</term>, Crocodiles teares, to signifie such teares as are fained, and spent onely with intent to deceiue, or doe harme. I saw once one of these beasts in London brought thither dead, but in perfect forme, of about three yards long.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="degree(n)">&#x00B0; Degree.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A terme often vsed in Astronomie and phisicke. In Astronomie it signifieth the thirtieth part of a signe: viz. of Aries, Taurus, Gemini, &amp;c. For in&shy;
to so many parts or de&shy;
grees are all the signes di&shy;
uided. In phisicke it sig&shy;
nifieth
</page>

<page sig="e7r">
a proportion of heat, cold, moisture or dri&shy;
nesse, in the nature of simples; and there are foure such proportions or degrees: The first degree is so smal, that it can scarce be perceiued. The second, that which may be mani&shy;
festly perceiued without hurting the sense. The third, that which some&shy;
what offendeth the sense. The fourth, which so much offendeth, that it may destroy the body. For example: Sweet Almonds Rice, Buglosse, ripe Grapes are hot in the first degree: Parsley, Saffron, Hony, in the second degree; Cum&shy;
mine, Galingall, Pepper in the third degree: and Garlick, Spourge, Euphor&shy;
bium, in the fourth degree. So Barly is cold in the first degree, cucumbers in the second, Sengreene in the third, and Hemlocke in the fourth degree. Where note that in heat, cold, and drinesse, there may bee foure degrees, and in moi&shy;
sture but two.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dialect(n)">Dialect.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A difference of some words, or pronunci&shy;

</page>

<page sig="f1r">
North, is different from that in the South, and the Western Dialect differing from them both. The Grecians had fiue especiall Dialects: as 1. The pro&shy;
perty of speech in Athens: 2. In Ionia: 3. In Doris: 4. In Eolia: and 5. that manner of speech which was generally vsed of them all. So euery countrey hath commonly in diuers parts thereof some differance of language, which is cal&shy;
led the Dialect of that place.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dictator(n)">Dictator.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A chiefe Offi&shy;
cer among the Romanes; neuer chosen but vpon great necessity in dange&shy;
rous troubles of the com&shy;
mon wealth. This Dictator could continue in office but onely sixe moneths, and then vnder paine of treason was to giue vp his authoritie: He could bee named by none but the Consull, and that in the night time, with great si&shy;
lence and attention. After he was once chosen all o&shy;
ther Magistrates were out of office, except onely the Tribune of the people; so that the Dictator, during his time, had (as it were) a kingly authority aboue all.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="divination(n)">Diuination</form> <xpln lang="en"> A foretel&shy;
ling of a thing before it happeneth: which may (as I take it) bee diui&shy;
ded into three different kindes; namely, super&shy;
naturall, naturall, and su&shy;
perstitious Diuination. Supernaturall Diuination
</page>

<page sig="f3v">
(onely reuealed to man by God,) is not properly called Diuination, but prophecy, with which all the holy Prophets haue in former times beene inspi&shy;
red. Naturall Diuination may bee diuided into two branches; whereof the first is, that which hath in former times been pra&shy;
ctised by wicked spirits in oracles and answers giuen by them in Idols, and is at this day sometime seene in possessed persons, who by suggestion of the diuell may foretell often things to come: and this is but a naturall Diuination: For although to vs it seeme miraculous, because of our ignorance in the causes and course of things, yet in those spirits, it is but natu&shy;
rall, who by their long ex&shy;
perience, and great obser&shy;
uation, besides the know&shy;
ledge of secrets in nature, and their quicke intelligence from all places, are able to foresee much more then wee by nature can. The second branch of na&shy;
turall Diuination, is that which a wise man may foretell by probable con&shy;
iecture, beeing no way of&shy;
fensiue, so long as it is on&shy;
ly guided by reason, and ouerruled by submitting it selfe to the almightie power of GOD. And to this second kinde of Diuination, may also A&shy;
strologie bee referred (which by the motion and influence of Starres and Planets doth pro&shy;
mise to foretell many things) so long as it kee&shy;
peth it selfe in due limits, and arrogateth not too much to the certainetie thereof: into which ex&shy;
cesse of vanity if it should breake foorth, it can then bee no longer called na&shy;
turall Diuination, but superstitious and wicked: for the Starres may in&shy;
cline but not impose a necessitie in particular things. The third and last manner of Diuinati&shy;
on, is that which we cal&shy;
led superstitious, whereof there hath among the Gentiles beene diuers dif&shy;
ferent kinds, namely. Au&shy;
</page>

<page sig="f4r">
guration, Aruspicie, Necro&shy;
mancie, Geomancie, Hydro&shy;
mancie, Pyromancie, Cossi&shy;
nomancie, Palmistrie or Chi&shy;
romancie.

Augutation, was a diui&shy;
nation made of things to come, by the flying, fee&shy;
ding, &amp; chirping of birds: the professors wherof cal&shy;
led Augurs, were of great account among the heath<expan type="e+_">en</expan> Romans, in so much that there was a Colledge of them in the citie; neither would the Romans vnder&shy;
take any publike matter of importance, without as&shy;
king their assent. But the vanity hereof was well de&shy;
rided by a wise Iew, named Mossolamus; as Iosephus writes. For an Augur in the wars once requiring, that the army which was then marching, might stand still a while, till he tooke obser&shy;
uation of a bird thereby, to foreknow the successe of that expedition, this Iew whilst the Augur was bu&shy;
sie in his art, shot at the Bird with an arrow, and by chance killed her: whereat the Augur and others being highly offen&shy;
ded: Are you so foolish (quoth the Iew) to ima&shy;
gine, this poore Bird can tell what will happen to vs, that could not foresee her owne death so neere at hand? Aruspicie, is a diuination, which by ope&shy;
ning and viewing the bow&shy;
els of beasts, did vnder&shy;
take to foretell things to come: the professors whereof were called A&shy;
ruspices. Necromancie, the worst of all others, is that diuination, which is practised by coniuration, and calling vp Diuels or dead mens ghosts. Which manner of diuination we reade practised by King Saul (1. Reg. cap. 28.) when he required a Sor&shy;
ceresse to call vp the spirit of Samuell to him. Geo&shy;
mancie, is a kinde of diui&shy;
nation practised by ma&shy;
king prickes and lines in the earth; as the name in Greeke signifieth. So Hy&shy;
dromancie, is a diuination made by some apparition in water, as Varro writeth that a Boy saw in water,
</page>

<page sig="f4v">
one bearing the forme of Mercurie, who foretold in one hundred and fiftie verses, the euent of the warre which the Ro&shy;
mans had with King Mithridates. Pyromancie is a diuination made by the fire, or spirits ap&shy;
pearing in the fire. Cos&shy;
cinomancie is a ridiculous kinde of diuination made with a sieue; which at this day is vsed by some sim&shy;
ple women, and appea&shy;
reth to bee of antiquitie, for in the third Idylle of Theocritus there is men&shy;
tion made hereof. Palmistrie or Chiromancie, is a diuination practised, by looking vpon the lines of the fingers and hands, an art still in vse, among fortune tellers, Egypti&shy;
ans, and iuglers. Besides these there were also o&shy;
ther diuinations, as name&shy;
ly Aeromancie, that which is gathered by apparitions in the aire. Capnomancie, by the flying of smoake. Catoptromancie by visions shewne in a glasse. All which being euen by the Pagans themselues ac&shy;
counted deceitfull and vaine, it remaineth that of Christians they be vtterly reiected and abhorred.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="divorce(n)">Diuorce.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A separation of man and wife, which was (as our Sauiour witnes&shy;
seth) first permitted by Moses vnto the Israelites, for the hardnesse of their hearts, that men might rather put their wiues a&shy;
way, whome they grew wearie of, then vse them with too great extremitie, to shorten their liues, as many did. The woman so diuorced was to haue of her husband, a writing (as Iosephus witnesseth to this effect. I promise, that here&shy;
after, I will lay no claime to thee: And this writing was called a bil of diuorce. But with Christians this custome is abrogated, sa&shy;
uing onely in case of a&shy;
dulterie. The auncient Romanes also had a cu&shy;
stome of diuorce, and a&shy;
mongst them, it was as lawfull for the wiues to put away their husbands, as for the husband to dis&shy;
misse
</page>

<page sig="f5r">
his wife: but amongst the Israelites, this prero&shy;
gatiue was onely permit&shy;
ted to the husband.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dolphin(n)">Dolphine.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A fish friendly to man, and especially to children; the Females of this fish, haue breasts like to women, which are well stored with milke. They are very faithfull to one another, and bring foorth yong ones like whelpes, after tenne moneths, and in Sommer time. They sometime breake foorth of the Sea, but presently die as soone as they touch land.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="druid(n)">Druides.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Ancient Pa&shy;

nation, and a great penal&shy;
tie laid on such as disobei&shy;
ed their sentence. They be&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ebony(n)">Ebene.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A tree which groweth in Ethiopia, bea&shy;
ting neither leaues nor fruite. It is blacke and hath no grain like other wood, and is sharp byting in tast: Being burned it yieldeth a pleasant smell, neither is the smoake thereof offen&shy;
siue: but the greene wood is so full of sap, that it will flame like a candle. It is good against many disea&shy;
ses of the eies. That which groweth in India, is spot&shy;
ted with white and yellow, being not in such estima&shy;
tion as the Ethiopian E&shy;
bene is.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="eclipse(n)">Eclipse.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A fayling or want of any thing: Com&shy;
monly
</page>

<page sig="f6v">
it signifieth a want of light, and there be two such Eclipses, namely of the Moone and of the Sunne. Eclipse of the Moone; neuer happeneth but at the full Moone, nei&shy;
ther then alwayes, but wh<expan type="e+_">en</expan> she is in such a point, that the shadow of the earth de&shy;
priueth her of the Sunne beames, from whence she taketh her light, Eclipse of the Sunne is not so vsuall and happeneth only at the change of the Moone, namely when the Moone being betweene the Sunne and vs, doth with her dark body, hide part of her light from vs: which was the cause that Dionysius Areo&shy;
pagita, seeing the Sunne so admirably eclipsed at our Sauiours passion, con&shy;
trary to all reason, when the Moone was not in any neerenesse to hinder his light, cryed out in amaze&shy;
ment: <term lang="la">Aut Deus naturae patitur, aut machina mundi dissoluetur</term>. Either the God of nature suffereth, or else the frame of the world wil be destroyed.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ecliptic line(n)">Ecliptike line.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An ima&shy;
gined line, running tho&shy;
rough the middest of the twelue signes, in which the sunne alwayes keepeth his course.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="eclogue(n)">Eclog.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It is commonly taken for a poeme contei&shy;

thering of things toge&shy;
ther.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="enthymeme(n)">Enthymeme.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A terme of Logick: It signifieth an im&shy;
perfect syllogisme, which wanteth either the Maior or Minor: as for example.
<lemeformat>
Euery sinne deserueth correction.
Euery theft is a sinne.
Therefore euery theft deserueth correction.
</lemeformat>
Now if wee will leaue
</page>

<page sig="g1r">
the first part (called the Maior) and say thus: Euery theft is a sinne: Therefore Euery theft deserueth cor&shy;
rection. Or omit the second part (named the Minor) and say: Euery sinne deser&shy;
ueth correction, therefore euery theft deserueth cor&shy;
rection: Then it is called an Enthymeme, to wit, a keeping in the minde (for so the word properly sig&shy;
nifieth) because one of these parts is vnderstood in the minde: where note that if the two ends of the Enthymeme are like in speech, then the Minor is wanting, if the two be&shy;
ginnings be like, the Ma&shy;
ior is omitted, as may ea&shy;
sily appeare by the forego&shy;
ing example.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="epact(n)">Epact.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A number which is in vse euery yeare, to finde the age of the Moone by: This Epact changeth year&shy;
ly, and is made by the ad&shy;
dition of eleuen to the for&shy;
mer Epact; both which numbers are the Epact for the following yeare, but alwaies so, that both those numbers exceede not the number of 30. For if they amount to aboue 30. then must you cast away the 30 and the remaining num&shy;
ber shall be the Epact. As for example, in this yeare 1616. the Epact is 22. to which if you adde eleuen for the next yeares Epact, it maketh 33. from which if you take away 30. there will remaine three; which is the Epact for the next yeare 1617. Note also: wh<expan type="e+_">en</expan> in any yeere the Epact is 19. you must adde 12. to find out the next yeares true Epact, that casting a&shy;
way thirty, the Epact may come to be eleuen In al o&shy;
ther n<expan type="o+_">om</expan>bers the Epact is stil made (as before) by adding
</page>

<page sig="g1v">
eleuen. And these Epacts are euer changed on the first day of March.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ephemeris(n)">Ephemerides.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A booke wherein day-acts are regi&shy;
stred. Commonly it is ta&shy;
ken for a Booke of Astro&shy;
nomy (in vse among such as erect figures to cast mens natiuities) by which booke is shewen how all the Planets are placed, e&shy;
uery day and houre of the yeare.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ephah(n)">Ephi.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An Hebrew mea&shy;
sure conteining about fiue peckes of ours.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ephod(n)">Ephod.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A holy garment worne by the high Priest of the Hebrewes, when he executed his function. It couered the backe, and was curiously wrought with gold and twisted silke of Purple, Scarlet, and Vio&shy;
let colour. On the shoul&shy;
ders there were fit two great precious Onix stones, and in them grauen the names of the twelue sonnes of Iacob, called the twelue Patriarches, in the right shoulder the sixe el&shy;
dest, and in the left the sixe youngest; that the High Priest entering into the San<emend err="it">ct</emend>um Sanctorium (which was the holiest place of the Temple (might beare with him the names of the peo&shy;
ple, for whom hee was to pray vnto God.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="epicycle(n)">Epicycle.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A terme vsed in Astronomy. It signifieth a lesser circle, whose cen&shy;
ter or middle part is in the circumference of a greater circle. In the vpper part of this Epicycle, the fiue Pla&shy;
nets Saturne, Iupiter, Mars, Venus, and Mercurie, doe goe forward according to the course of the signes; as Aries to Taurus, &amp;c. in the lower part they are re&shy;
trograde, that is, goe back&shy;
ward, as from Gemini to Taurus, from Taurus to Aries againe. Betweene these two motions, are said to bee two stations; namely when a Planet ceaseth going forward, and beginneth to be retro&shy;
grade, or comming to the farthest point of his retro&shy;
gradation, goeth forward againe: so that in the Epi&shy;
cycle, these Planets wheele about somtime according,
</page>

<page sig="g2r">
sometime contrary to the order of the signes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="epicure(n)">Epicure.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It is commonly taken with vs, for a man giuen ouer much to plea&shy;
sure, especially in gluttony. In ancient times it signifi&shy;
ed one that followed the sect of a Philosopher, na&shy;
med Epicurus, who taught that the greatest happines was, to bee without paine, and enioy pleasure of body and minde.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="epigram(n)">Epigramme.</form> <xpln lang="en"> It pro&shy;
perly signifieth a super&shy;
scription or writing set vpon any thing; now it is c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>monly taken for a short wittie poeme, which vnder a fained name, doth co&shy;
uertly praise or taxe some particular person or thing.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="epilepsy(n)">Epilepsie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The falling sicknesse, whereto most commonly children and yong folk are subiect. This disease is caused by some humor or vapor, suddenly stopping the passage of spi&shy;
rits in the braine, which the brain striuing to excel causeth the patient to fall downe, and commonly fome at the mouth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="epilogue(n)">Epilog.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The conclusion or end of a matter. A speech made, after an enterlude or play is ended.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="epiphany(n)">Epiphanie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An appearing or manifestati<expan type="o+_">on</expan>. The feast of Twelfe day at Christ&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Graces(n)">Graces.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A poeticall fi&shy;
ction of three sisters, sup&shy;
posed the Daughters of Iupiter and Venus. They were called Aglaia, Thalia, and Euphorsyne. The mo&shy;
rality of this inuention was, to expresse the mutual loue and cheerefull con&shy;
uersation which ought to be amongst friends: For they were painted naked, to signifie that friendship ought to be plain without dissimulation; smiling and
</page>

<page sig="h3r">
merry, to shew that men should do good willingly; yong &amp; maid<expan type="e+_">en</expan>like, to teach that friendship should con&shy;
sist in honest things; and holding hands together in a round ring, to shew that a benefite bestowed, doth againe returne to the gi&shy;
uer.</xpln></wordentry>

gle, but of purple colour, with red fierie eyes, and whitish wings, and in the hinder part blacke, made much like a Lyon. This bird neasteth in the high mountaines, and is excee&shy;
ding hard to be taken, ex&shy;
cept very young, for he wil aduenture on any man, and is so fierce, that hee often killeth Elephants &amp; Dra&shy;
gons. Hee is most enemy to horses: for which cause Virgill in his eight Eclog, bringeth in the shepheard Damon, who wondered at a strange marriage, speaking thus: <term lang="la">Iungunturiam gry&shy;
phes equis, &amp;c.</term> The Grif&shy;
fins fierce are ioyned with horses now.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="harpy(n)">Harpyes.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Poeticall word. It signifieth certaine monstrous birds, with maiden faces, &amp; crooked sharp talons, so called because of their rapine which they vsed. They liued in Stym&shy;

</page>

<page sig="h4v">
the meate of Phineus, king of that countrey, because he at the perswasion of his second wife, had destroyed the children which he had by his first; they were at last driuen away by Zetus, and Calais the sonnes of Boreas. These Harpyes were named, Aello, Ocypete, Celeno, and Thyella.
</xpln></wordentry>

ten, and some bleede not at all. 
They are caused by superfluitie of grosse me&shy;
lancholy blood, sent to those parts from the li&shy;
uer, beeing many times healthfull to the patient, by preuenting other disea&shy;
ses; 
and therefore ought not hastily to be stopt, vn&shy;
lesse through continu&shy;
ance, the body bee too much weakened by them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hierarchy(n)">Hierarchie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The holy or&shy;

nitie, there being in nine, thrice three, and in euery three thrice one. So that there are three superi&shy;
our, three inferiour, and three middle degrees. The superiour are Sera&shy;
phines, Cerubines, and Thrones; the middle, Dominations, principalities, Powers: The inferiour, vertues, Archangels, and Angels.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hieroglyphics(n)">Hieroglyphikes.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A darke mysticall kind of writing, vsed chiefely in times past among the Pagan priests and learned men of Egypt to hide their knowledge from the vulgar sort. This writing was by making the formes of beasts and di&shy;
uers other figures, and could hardly bee vnder&shy;
stood without exposition, or great knowledge in the nature of things. For ex&shy;
ample: Eternity or Euerla&shy;
stingnesse, they expressed by a round circle, which hath no end: A king, by a scepter with an eye in the toppe thereof: A mat&shy;
ter of haste, by a Dolphin, the swiftest of all fishes. And a matter of delibera&shy;
tion or aduice, by an Anchor, which stayeth a ship in the waues. With a thou&shy;
sand such deuices, not sub&shy;
iect to common capaci&shy;
ties to finde out.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="homage(n)">Homage.</form> <xpln lang="en"> In Court Ba&shy;
rons, the Iury that are sworne to enquire of mat&shy;
ters, are so called. It signifi&shy;
eth also a seruil ceremony, of duty by some Tenants to their Lords, after this manner. The Tenant that holdeth Landes by ho&shy;
mage, kneeling on both knees before the Lord (who sitteth and holdeth the Tenants hands be&shy;
tween his) saith as follow&shy;
eth. I become your man, from this day forward, of life and member, and of earthly honour, and to you shall bee faithfull and true, and shall beare to you faith for the Lands I claime to holde of you, sauing the faith that I owe to our Lord the King. And then the Lord sitting must kisse the Tenant.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hodge-podge(n)">Hotchpot</form> <xpln lang="en"> A terme in our common Lawe, when a daughter which hath had giuen vnto her any lands in Frankemarriage, clay&shy;
ming to bee coheire after her fathers death, to other lands with some sisters, is constrained to suffer that part of land, giuen her before her fathers death, to bee put in Hotchpot, that is, to be mingled to&shy;
gether with the landes whereof her Father dyed seised, so that an equall di&shy;
uision may be made of the whole.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Ides(n)">Ides.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Eight daies in euery moneth, so called, because they diuide the moneth al&shy;

nue to the fifteenth: in o&shy;
ther months they begin at the sixt day, and continue to the thirteenth. Where note that the last day onely is called Ides, and the first of these dayes, the eighth Ides, the second the seuenth Ides: that is, the eighth or seuenth day before the Ides, and so of the rest.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="jubilee(n)">Iubilie.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A publike reioy&shy;
cing or a great shout for ioy. Among the Hebrewes euery fiftith a yeare was cal&shy;
led the yeare of Iubile; for then were bond men of their owne country made free, possessions returned againe to the first owners, neither was it lawfull to plant or sowe any thing that yeare. Among Chri&shy;
stians this solemnitie of keeping a yeare of Iubilie, was first instituted by Bo&shy;
nifacius the eight, in the yeare of our Lord. 1300. who ordayned that it should bee obserued euery hundreth yeare. After this Clement the sixt, instituted it to be kept euery fiftieth yeare. And lastly, Sixtus the fourth brought it to bee celebrated euery fiue and twentieth yeare, be&shy;
ginning it first in the yeare of our Lord God, 1475.</xpln></wordentry>

If these plumbes bee kept long, they waxe dry, and full of wrinckles. They are temperate in heate and cold, and are good against the cough, roughnes of the throate, and against all ex&shy;
ulcerations and inflamma&shy;
tions of the kidness and bladder: but beeing eaten for meate, they are of hard digestion.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="labyrinth(n)">Labyrinth.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An intricate building or place made with so many turnings and windings, that whosoeuer went into it, could neuer get out without a perfect guide, or a thred to direct him, the ende of which thred must be tyed at the doore where hee entreth. Some heathen Princes bestowed infinite charge, to build such deuices in a stately forme, There were foure principall labyrinths in the world. The first in Egypt, made for a sepul&shy;
cher of one of their Kings or (as some write) in ho&shy;
nour of the sunne. The se&shy;
cond in the yland of Can&shy;
die, built at the comman&shy;
dement of King Minos, by the ingenious workeman Daedalus, who tooke his patte<emend err="t">r</emend>ne, from that which he had seene in Egypt, the third in the yle Lemnos, the fourth in Italy, built by King Porsenna, of great square stones, for his own sepulcher.</xpln></wordentry>

tles, it doth strengthen the skinne, and keepeth haire from falling off.</xpln></wordentry>

of the first band conteined 1105. footemen, and 123. horsemen; in which band the Standard was alwaies carried. The other nine bands had euery one 555. footemen, and 66. horse&shy;
men; so that a Legion made vp the number of 6100. footmen, and 726. horse&shy;
men.</xpln></wordentry>

It is written notwithstan&shy;
ding, thru beeing rubbed with the iuyce of Gar&shy;
licke, it cannot then draw Iron, as likewise if a Dia&shy;
mond bee laide close vnto it. This stone is found in the Indian Sea, and also in the Countrey of Tra&shy;
chonitis: It is of greatest vse in Nauigation, for by it Saylers finde out the certaine course of their voyage; the needle (in their compasse) tempered herewith, still standing di&shy;
rectly toward the North and South.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mandrake(n)">Mandrak.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A strange hearbe, bearing yellowe round Apples. The roote of this hearbe it great and white like a Radish roote, and is diuided into two or moe parts, growing often almost like to the legs of a man. This roote, espe&shy;
cially the barke thereof, is extremely cold and drie, euen to the fourth degree: It is therefore ve&shy;
ry dangerous to receiue inwardly, for that the least quantitie too much, will quickly kill one. Surge&shy;
ons vse to steepe this root in Wine, and giue it to bee drunken of such as they must cut, sawe, or burne in any part, for the colde operation thereof causeth sleepe, and maketh the body insensible for a time.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="manna(n)">Manna.</form> <xpln lang="en"> In holy Scrip&shy;
ture it signifieth a delicate food, which God sent from heauen to the Israe&shy;
lites, in manner of a dew, white and somewhat like Coriander seede: with which the Israelites liued fortie yeares in the Wil&shy;
dernesse. till they came to the borders of the land of Canaan. At the first
</page>

<page sig="k4r">
sending hereof, the peo&shy;
ple were in such admirati&shy;
on, that they said to each other, Manhu? viz. What is this? Which seemeth to bee the cause, why it was after called Manna. In Physicke it is taken for a kinde of dew, which fal&shy;
ling in hotte countries vp&shy;
on trees, doth there con&shy;
geale, almost like to crummes of whitebread, and is gathered &amp; choice&shy;
ly kept, as a gentle purger of choler.</xpln></wordentry>

site to bee any stone out of which, fire may bee stric&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="margarite(n)">Margarites.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Little pearles found in the shell fish, e&shy;
specially in Oysters, wher&shy;
of some haue holes in them, and some haue none: the best are brought out of India, yet they are also found in our English Seas, as also in the Fle&shy;
mish and Almaine, and the fairest Margarits grow in yong shels. Some write that in Thunder, the Oy&shy;
sters doe cast them vp, as it were in way of aborti&shy;
on, which is the cause that they are often found in the sands. They are some&shy;
times vsed by Physitions in cordiall confections, for they comfort the spirits, and are therefore good a&shy;
gainst swoundings hauing vertue also in them to stop issues of blood, or any loosenesse of the bo&shy;
die.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="microcosmos(n)">Microcosmus.</form> <xpln lang="en">
It properly signifieth a little world.

cosmus or little world, be&shy;
cause his body being com&shy;
pared to the baser part of the world, and his soule to, the blessed Angels, see&shy;
meth to signifie, that man is as it were a little world and that the whole world doeth resemble a great man.

They also cut not their haire, but suffered it still to grow, and obser&shy;
ued diuers other ceremo&shy;
nies. The date of their vowe being expired, they presented themselues to
</page>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Olympic Games(n)">Olympicke games.</form> <xpln lang="en"> So&shy;

nesse, rather then vertue should make men striue for victorie: which was the cause that Tigranes Sonne of Artabanus, ser&shy;
uing in the huge armie of King Xerxes, who then in&shy;
uaded Greece, said to Mar&shy;
donius, who had perswaded the King to that enter&shy;
prize; against what a peo&shy;
ple haue you brought vs to fight, that more esteeme honour then mony? Thin&shy;
king it impossible to ouer&shy;
come such Nations as were so affected.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="oracle(n)">Oracle.</form> <xpln lang="en"> An answere or counsel giuen by God. A&shy;
mong the Gentiles, these Oracles were but illusions of the diuel, who answered for the most part doubt&shy;
fully in Idols, to questions or demands made vnto him. There were two such principall places of Oracles: One of Ammon or Iupiter, in Lybia, which was founded vpon this oc&shy;
casion. When Bacchus after his conquests in India, came into the hot burning countrey of Lybia, and wanted there water to re&shy;
leeue his armie, it is said that he praied to his father Iupiter to helpe him in that distresse: whereupon there appeared a Ramme vnto him, which stamping vpon the ground with his foote, caused a fountaine forth&shy;
with to spring in the place. Bacchus obtaining by this meanes his desire, built a temple there of excee&shy;
ding huge greatnesse, in the middle whereof, hee erected an Idoll to Iupiter, in the likenesse of a Ram; which Idol afterward gaue Oracles, and was called the Oracle of Ammon; be&shy;
cause it was situated in a dry sandy place; for <term lang="gkt">Am&shy;
mos</term> in Greeke signifieth Sand. The other Oracle was at Delphi, a Cittie of Beotia in Greece, where Apollo was said to giue an&shy;
swers.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="eryngo(n)">Oringos.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Certaine rootes growing in some places, neere the Sea side. The Plant is called Sea hollie, bearing prickly broad leaues, and round sharpe prickly heads, set about with small skie coloured floures. These rootes are very long and deepe into the ground, of an ashie co&shy;
lour in the outside and full of ioynts. They vse, scra&shy;
ping of the outer rinde of these roots, &amp; pulling out the pith, to preserue them by boyling them with Su&shy;
gar or Hony, &amp; a little Ci&shy;
namon and Ginger; which preserue is very good for a&shy;
ged people, and such as are consumed in their bodie, to nourish them againe.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="palm(n)">Palme.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The tree which beareth Dates, growing plentifully in the holy land. There are of these trees found also in some parts of Egypt, but they beare no fruite, or if they beare any it is vnpleasant. The branches of this tree, were wont to be carried as a token of victory, because they are of that nature, that they wil stil shoot vpward, though oppressed with ne&shy;
uer so great weight, &amp; the leaues thereof neuer fall. Of this tree there is male and female: the male bea&shy;
reth only blossomes &amp; no fruit, but the female beares both. In olde times, some people vsed to write with Paper, made of leaues of the Palme tree.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="panther(n)">Panther.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A fierce wilde
</page>

<page sig="l7r">
beast, hauing a sweet smel, and a faire spotted skinne, wherewith shee allureth other beastes to looke on her; hiding her head least it should make them afraid, and by this means, getteth her prey more easily. The male of this beast is the li&shy;
bard. The panthers (as is written) haue on their shoulder a spotte, which groweth and waineth like the moon. This beast is so fearfull of the Hyena, that in his presence shee dareth not doe any thing: in so much that if one haue but a peece of the skinne of a Hyena, the Panther will not touch him, and it is sayde, that if both their skins be hanged together, the haire of the Panthers skin will fall of.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Paralipomenon(n)">Paralipomenon.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Omitted, or not spoken of: There are two bookes in the old testam<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t so called, because many worthy histories o&shy;
mitted in the bookes of Kings, are there related.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="parallel(n)">Parallels.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Lines running of an equall distance from each other, which can ne&shy;
uer meete, though they be drawn infinitely in length, thus, ==========
</page>

<page sig="l7v">
In Astronomie there are fiue such imagined lines, running circlewise about the round compasse of the heavens. The first is the Equinoctiall line iust in the middle of the world betweene the two Poles. The second northward from the Equinoctiall, is the Tropike of Cancer, to which line the sunne com&shy;
meth about the twelfth day of Iune. The third (yet more northward) is the northerne circle, with&shy;
in 23. degrees and 50. mi&shy;
nutes of the North pole. The fourth line is the Tropike of Capricorne, de&shy;
clining southward fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> the Equinoctiall, as much as the Tropike of Cancer doth northward, and to this line the sunne commeth about the twelfth of December. The fift and last line is the southerne circle, beeing as neere the south pole, as the northerne circle (before spoken of) is to the North pole.
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="paralogism(n)">Paralogisme.</form> <xpln lang="en">
A deceitfull syllogisme, a manner of ar&shy;
guing which seemeth true, when it is not as in saying, Hee that affirmeth William to be a liuing creature, sayth true.
He that affirmeth William to bee a horse, affirmeth him to be a liuing creature.
Therefore he that affirmeth William to bee a horse saith true.
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="paramount(n)">Paramounte.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The chiefe Lord of the fee.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="paramour(n)">Paramour.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Sweete heart, one dearely belo&shy;
ued.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="paraphrase(n)">Paraphrase.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A free man&shy;
ner of translation or inter&shy;
pretation, wherein a man doth not tye himselfe to expresse euery word as it lyeth in the coppy, but to explicate and adorne the matter more at large, and to abridge somethings, yet still keeping the sense of the Author, any such tran&shy;
slati<expan type="o+_">on</expan> is called a Paraphra&shy;
sticall translation.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="parasite(n)">Parasite.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A Flatterer, a trencher friend. One that is still hanging on some rich man, and flatteringly feedeth his humor because hee would bee partaker of his good cheere.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="phoenix(n)">Phenix.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The rarest Bird in the world. It is written, that there was neuer any but one of this kind liuing at one time, and that only in Arabia; of the bignesse of an Eagle, of a purple colour, hauing a bright colour of golde about his necke, a goodly faire taile, and a tuft of feathers vpon his head. He liueth aboue 600. yeares, and being old, buildeth him a nest of Cinnamon and the twigs of Frankincence, which he filleth with spices, &amp; then with the labouring of his wings in the Sun, setting it on fire, is there consumed in it; out of whose Ashes there groweth a Worme, and of the worme another Phenix.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="philosophy(n)">Philosophy.</form> <xpln lang="en"> The study of wisedome: a deepe know&shy;

cluding, Grammar, Logik, and Rhetoricke. 2 Naturall Philosophy teaching the nature of all things, and conteining besides Arith&shy;
metick, Musick, Geometry and Astronomy. 3. Morall Philosophy, which consi&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="predicament(n)">Predicament.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A terme of Logicke: It signifieth a different order in the na&shy;
ture of things, or certaine generall heads, to which they may be referred; and there are c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>only recko&shy;
ned ten such predicaments The first called Substance, includeth all substances whatsoeuer, as the foure Elements, and all other creatures. The second na&shy;
med Quantity, containeth all quantities, as ten, twen&shy;
ty, a yard, a furlong, a mile. The third called Quality, hath vnder it all qualities, as wisedome, art, fortitude, diligence, sloth. The fourth named Relation, is proper&shy;
ly of such words, as depend mutually one vpon ano&shy;
ther, as a husband and wife, a master and seruant, a father and child. The o&shy;
ther sixe are, 5. Action or doing: 6. Passion or suffe&shy;
ring: 7. Where: 8. When: 9. Situation or placing: 10. the Habit or outward co&shy;
uering of a thing.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="predicate(v)">Predicate.</form> <xpln lang="en"> To tell abroad, to report. In Logicke, it signifieth the later part of a proposition, as in saying, Paul is an Apostle: The word Apostle is called pre&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sib(n)">Sibbe.</form> <xpln lang="en"> One of kinne.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sibyl(n)">Sybill.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A woman inspi&shy;
red with a spirit of prophe&shy;
sie; so called of the greeke words <term lang="gkt">Sios</term>, which signifi&shy;
eth God, and <term lang="gkt">Boyle</term>, which signifieth counsell, because such women had know&shy;
ledge (as was saide) of the counsell of God. There were tenne Sybils, famous aboue the rest. The first was Sybilla of Persia, the second of Lybia, the third of the City Delphos in Greece, the fourth of Cunia, a city in Aeolis, the <emend err="first">fifth</emend> of Erythrae, a city of Asia, the sixt of the Ile, Sa&shy;
mos, the seauenth of Cu&shy;
mae, a city of Campania in Italy. This Sibylla of Cumae, (as it is written) came on a time to Tarquine king of Rome, in the habit of a strange olde woman, offering to sell him nine bookes, full (as she said) of diuine oracles, for which shee demanded three hun&shy;
dred crownes of golde: the King not much regarding, and besides thinking them too deere at that price, she burned three of them be&shy;
fore his face, and then as&shy;
ked if hee would haue the other sixe, for which she demaunded no lesse, than she had done at first for the nine. Hereat the King de&shy;
</page>

<page sig="n8r">
riding her and thinking her halfe made, she burned three more of them, and tolde him that he should giue her the same price for those three that were left. The King much wonde&shy;
ring at the womans con&shy;
stant earnestnesse, and therefore thinking the books contained no com&shy;
mon matter, commanded three hundred crownes to bee giuen her for them, which she receiuing, pre&shy;
sently vanished out of sight. These bookes were after kept by the Romans very carefully, who in great matter of doubt, alwaies had recourse to them, as to an assured Oracle. The eighth Sibyll, was called Sibylla of Helespontus in Greece; the ninth was of Phrygia; the tenth and last of Tybur, a citie neere Rome in Italy. All these Sibylls prophecied of the incarnation of our Sauiour Christ.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vestal(n)">Vestals.</form> <xpln lang="en"> Certaine virgins among the ancient Ro&shy;
manes, consecrated to the Goddesse Vesta. They were alwayes chosen be&shy;
tweene sixe and tenne yeares of age, and conti&shy;
nued thirty yeeres in their office; whereof the
</page>

<page sig="p1v">
first tenne yeares they be&shy;
stowed in learning the ce&shy;
remonies of their order, the second they employed in execution thereof, and the last tenne in teaching others, after it was law&shy;
full for them to marry if they would. Their chiefe office was to keepe fire continually burning in a round temple at Rome in honour of Ve&shy;
sta, and if it chanced to goe out, they were to renew it againe with no vsuall fire, but such as they could get by art from the Sunne beames. They were greatly honoured in the City, and had diuers priuiledges: for they were carried in Cha&shy;
riots, and the chiefest Magistrates would doe reuerence to them. They had officers going before them, as the Consuls had, and if they met any who was ledde to bee put to death, they had authori&shy;
ty to deliuer him, taking an oath that they came not that way of purpose, but by ch<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ce. They might also make a will, and dis&shy;
pose of their goods as they pleased. But if any of them were found to liue vnchast shee was openly carryed with sad silence to the gate called Collina, where being put into a deepe pitte, shee was presently buryed a&shy;
liue. These Vestals were first instituted by Numa Pompilius, or as some write, by Romulus.
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="zone(n)">Zone.</form> <xpln lang="en"> A girdle in Cos&shy;
mography, it signifieth a diuision made of the hea&shy;
uens into fiue parts, wher&shy;
of one is extreme hot, two extreame colde, and two temperate. The hot Zone, otherwise called the bur&shy;
ning Zone, is all that part of the heauens, which is contained betweene the two Tropickes of Cancer and Capricorne in which Zone, continually the Sun keepeth his course. The two cold Zones are vnder the two poles of the world, or within 23. de&shy;
grees neere them. The two temperate are the Zones betweene the far&shy;
thest extreme cold and the middle burning zone. And with these fiue Zones of the heauens, doeth the earth vnder, agree in heat, cold, temperature.</xpln></wordentry>
